    </div>

    <div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en">
     <div class="hatnote">
      For the most recent Summer Games in London, see
      <a href="/wiki/2012_Summer_Paralympics" title="2012 Summer Paralympics">
       2012 Summer Paralympics
      </a>
      . For the most recent Winter Games in Sochi, see
      <a href="/wiki/2014_Winter_Paralympics" title="2014 Winter Paralympics">
       2014 Winter Paralympics
      </a>
      .
     </div>
     <div class="hatnote">
      For the next Summer Games in Rio de Janeiro, see
      <a href="/wiki/2016_Summer_Paralympics" title="2016 Summer Paralympics">
       2016 Summer Paralympics
      </a>
      . For the next Winter Games in Pyeongchang, see
      <a href="/wiki/2018_Winter_Paralympics" title="2018 Winter Paralympics">
       2018 Winter Paralympics
      </a>
      .
     </div>

            </li>
            <li>
             <a href="/wiki/Deaflympics" title="Deaflympics">
              Deaflympics
             </a>
            </li>
            <li>
             <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Invictus_Games_(sport)" title="Invictus Games (sport)">
              Invictus Games
             </a>
            </li>
           </ul>
          </div>
         </div>
        </div>
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">
        <div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0">

           Culture
          </a>
         </div>
         <div class="NavContent" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center">

            <a href="/wiki/Disability_in_the_arts" title="Disability in the arts">

       <td style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;border-top:1px solid #bbf;padding-bottom:0.4em;border-bottom:1px solid #bbf;">
        <ul>
         <li>
          <span class="metadata">
           <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Handicapped_Accessible_sign.svg">
            <img alt="Portal icon" data-file-height="451" data-file-width="451" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Handicapped_Accessible_sign.svg/16px-Handicapped_Accessible_sign.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Handicapped_Accessible_sign.svg/24px-Handicapped_Accessible_sign.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Handicapped_Accessible_sign.svg/32px-Handicapped_Accessible_sign.svg.png 2x" width="16"/>
           </a>
          </span>
          <a href="/wiki/Portal:Disability" title="Portal:Disability">
           Disability portal
          </a>
         </li>
         <li>
          <div class="hlist">
           <ul>
            <li>
             <img alt="Category" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="36" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/48/Folder_Hexagonal_Icon.svg/16px-Folder_Hexagonal_Icon.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/48/Folder_Hexagonal_Icon.svg/24px-Folder_Hexagonal_Icon.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/48/Folder_Hexagonal_Icon.svg/32px-Folder_Hexagonal_Icon.svg.png 2x" title="Category" width="16"/>

      <tr>
       <td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;">
        <div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
         <ul>
          <li class="nv-view">
           <a href="/wiki/Template:Disability_sidebar" title="Template:Disability sidebar">
            <span title="View this template">
             v
            </span>
           </a>
          </li>
          <li class="nv-talk">
           <a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Disability_sidebar" title="Template talk:Disability sidebar">
            <span title="Discuss this template">
             t
            </span>
           </a>
          </li>
          <li class="nv-edit">
           <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Disability_sidebar&amp;action=edit">
            <span title="Edit this template">
             e
            </span>
           </a>
          </li>
         </ul>
        </div>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      The
      <b>
       Paralympic Games
      </b>
      is a major international
      <a href="/wiki/Multi-sport_event" title="Multi-sport event">
       multi-sport event
      </a>
      , involving athletes with a range of
      <a href="/wiki/Physical_disability" title="Physical disability">
       physical disabilities
      </a>
      , including impaired muscle power (e.g.
      <a href="/wiki/Paraplegia" title="Paraplegia">
       paraplegia
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quadriplegia" title="Quadriplegia">
       quadriplegia
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Muscular_dystrophy" title="Muscular dystrophy">
       muscular dystrophy
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Post-polio_syndrome" title="Post-polio syndrome">
       Post-polio syndrome
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Spina_bifida" title="Spina bifida">
       spina bifida
      </a>
      ), impaired passive range of movement, limb deficiency (e.g.
      <a href="/wiki/Amputation" title="Amputation">
       amputation
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Dysmelia" title="Dysmelia">
       dysmelia
      </a>
      ), leg length difference,
      <a href="/wiki/Short_stature" title="Short stature">
       short stature
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Hypertonia" title="Hypertonia">
       hypertonia
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Ataxia" title="Ataxia">
       ataxia
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Athetosis" title="Athetosis">
       athetosis
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vision_impairment" title="Vision impairment">
       vision impairment
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intellectual_impairment" title="Intellectual impairment">
       intellectual impairment
      </a>
      . There are
      <a href="/wiki/Winter_Paralympic_Games" title="Winter Paralympic Games">
       Winter
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Summer_Paralympic_Games" title="Summer Paralympic Games">
       Summer Paralympic Games
      </a>
      , which since the 1988 Summer Games in Seoul, South Korea, are held almost immediately following the respective
      <a href="/wiki/Olympic_Games" title="Olympic Games">
       Olympic Games
      </a>
      . All Paralympic Games are governed by the
      <a href="/wiki/International_Paralympic_Committee" title="International Paralympic Committee">
       International Paralympic Committee
      </a>
      (IPC).
     </p>
     <p>
      The Paralympics has grown from a small gathering of British
      <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">
       World War II
      </a>
      veterans in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stoke_Mandeville_Games" title="Stoke Mandeville Games">
       1948
      </a>
      to become one of the largest international sporting events by the early 21st century. Paralympians strive for equal treatment with non-disabled Olympic athletes, but there is a large funding gap between Olympic and Paralympic athletes.
     </p>
     <p>
      The Paralympic Games are organized in parallel with the
      <a href="/wiki/Olympic_Games" title="Olympic Games">
       Olympic Games
      </a>
      , while the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IOC" title="IOC">
       IOC
      </a>
      -recognized
      <a href="/wiki/Special_Olympics_World_Games" title="Special Olympics World Games">
       Special Olympics World Games
      </a>
      include athletes with
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intellectual_disabilities" title="Intellectual disabilities">
       intellectual disabilities
      </a>
      , and the
      <a href="/wiki/Deaflympics" title="Deaflympics">
       Deaflympics
      </a>
      include
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deaf" title="Deaf">
       deaf
      </a>
      athletes.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-1">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-specialolympics_2-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-specialolympics-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Given the wide variety of disabilities that Paralympic athletes have, there are several categories in which the athletes compete. The allowable disabilities are broken down into ten eligible impairment types. The categories are impaired muscle power, impaired passive range of movement, limb deficiency, leg length difference,
      <a href="/wiki/Short_stature" title="Short stature">
       short stature
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Hypertonia" title="Hypertonia">
       hypertonia
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Ataxia" title="Ataxia">
       ataxia
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Athetosis" title="Athetosis">
       athetosis
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vision_impairment" title="Vision impairment">
       vision impairment
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intellectual_impairment" title="Intellectual impairment">
       intellectual impairment
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      These categories are further broken down into classifications, which vary from sport to sport. The classification system has led to cheating controversies revolving around athletes who over-stated their disabilities, in addition to the use of performance-enhancing drugs seen in other events.
     </p>
     <p>
     </p>

     <p>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
       History
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Paralympic_Games&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Forerunners">
       Forerunners
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Paralympic_Games&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Forerunners">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote">
      Further information:
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_Wheelchair_and_Amputee_Games" title="World Wheelchair and Amputee Games">
       World Wheelchair and Amputee Games
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RUSMARKA-1777.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="700" data-file-width="700" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/RUSMARKA-1777.jpg/220px-RUSMARKA-1777.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/RUSMARKA-1777.jpg/330px-RUSMARKA-1777.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/RUSMARKA-1777.jpg/440px-RUSMARKA-1777.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RUSMARKA-1777.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Sir
        <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Guttmann" title="Ludwig Guttmann">
         Ludwig Guttmann
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Athletes with disabilities did compete in the Olympic Games prior to the advent of the Paralympics. The first athlete to do so was
      <a href="/wiki/German_American" title="German American">
       German American
      </a>
      gymnast
      <a href="/wiki/George_Eyser" title="George Eyser">
       George Eyser
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/1904_Summer_Olympics" title="1904 Summer Olympics">
       1904
      </a>
      , who had one artificial leg.
      <a href="/wiki/Hungary_at_the_1948_Summer_Olympics" title="Hungary at the 1948 Summer Olympics">
       Hungarian
      </a>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Karoly_Takacs" title="Karoly Takacs">
       Karoly Takacs
      </a>
      competed in shooting events in both the
      <a href="/wiki/1948_Summer_Olympics" title="1948 Summer Olympics">
       1948
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/1952_Summer_Olympics" title="1952 Summer Olympics">
       1952
      </a>
      Summer Olympics. He was a right-arm amputee and could shoot left-handed. Another disabled athlete to appear in the Olympics prior to the Paralympic Games was
      <a href="/wiki/Lis_Hartel" title="Lis Hartel">
       Lis Hartel
      </a>
      , a
      <a href="/wiki/Denmark_at_the_1952_Summer_Olympics" title="Denmark at the 1952 Summer Olympics">
       Danish
      </a>
      equestrian athlete who had contracted polio in 1943 and won a silver medal in the
      <a href="/wiki/Equestrian_at_the_1952_Summer_Olympics" title="Equestrian at the 1952 Summer Olympics">
       dressage
      </a>
      event.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-4">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The first organized athletic day for disabled athletes that coincided with the Olympic Games took place on the day of the opening of the
      <a href="/wiki/1948_Summer_Olympics" title="1948 Summer Olympics">
       1948 Summer Olympics
      </a>
      in London, United Kingdom.
      <a href="/wiki/Germans" title="Germans">
       German born
      </a>
      Dr.
      <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Guttmann" title="Ludwig Guttmann">
       Ludwig Guttmann
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Stoke_Mandeville_Hospital" title="Stoke Mandeville Hospital">
       Stoke Mandeville Hospital
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      who had been helped to flee Nazi Germany by the
      <a href="/wiki/Council_for_Assisting_Refugee_Academics" title="Council for Assisting Refugee Academics">
       Council for Assisting Refugee Academics
      </a>
      (CARA) in 1939,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-6">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        6
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      hosted a sports competition for British
      <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">
       World War II
      </a>
      veteran patients with spinal cord injuries. The first games were called the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_Wheelchair_and_Amputee_Games" title="World Wheelchair and Amputee Games">
       1948 International Wheelchair Games
      </a>
      , and were intended to coincide with the
      <a href="/wiki/1948_Summer_Olympics" title="1948 Summer Olympics">
       1948 Olympics
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-history_7-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-history-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Dr. Guttman's aim was to create an elite sports competition for people with disabilities that would be equivalent to the Olympic Games.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-history_7-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-history-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The games were held again at the same location in 1952, and Dutch veterans took part alongside the British, making it the first international competition of its kind. These early competitions, also known as the Stoke Mandeville Games, have been described as the precursors of the Paralympic Games.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Milestones">
       Milestones
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Paralympic_Games&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Milestones">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      There have been several milestones in the Paralympic movement. The first official Paralympic Games, no longer open solely to war veterans, was held in Rome in
      <a href="/wiki/1960_Summer_Paralympics" title="1960 Summer Paralympics">
       1960
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      400 athletes from 23 countries competed at the 1960 Games. Since 1960, the Paralympic Games have taken place in the same year as the Olympic Games.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-winterhistory_10-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-winterhistory-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
       <a href="#cite_note-11">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        11
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Games were initially open only to athletes in wheelchairs; at the
      <a href="/wiki/1976_Summer_Paralympics" title="1976 Summer Paralympics">
       1976 Summer Games
      </a>
      , athletes with different disabilities were included for the first time at a Summer Paralympics.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-history_7-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-history-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      With the inclusion of more disability classifications the 1976 Summer Games expanded to 1,600 athletes from 40 countries.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-winterhistory_10-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-winterhistory-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/1988_Summer_Paralympics" title="1988 Summer Paralympics">
       1988 Summer Paralympics
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Seoul" title="Seoul">
       Seoul
      </a>
      , South Korea, was another milestone for the Paralympic movement. It was in Seoul that the Paralympic Summer Games were held directly after the
      <a href="/wiki/1988_Summer_Olympics" title="1988 Summer Olympics">
       Olympic Summer Games
      </a>
      , in the same host city, and using the same facilities. This set a precedent that was followed in
      <a href="/wiki/1992_Summer_Paralympics" title="1992 Summer Paralympics">
       1992
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/1996_Summer_Paralympics" title="1996 Summer Paralympics">
       1996
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/2000_Summer_Paralympics" title="2000 Summer Paralympics">
       2000
      </a>
      . It was eventually formalized in an agreement between the
      <a href="/wiki/International_Paralympic_Committee" title="International Paralympic Committee">
       International Paralympic Committee
      </a>
      (IPC) and the
      <a href="/wiki/International_Olympic_Committee" title="International Olympic Committee">
       International Olympic Committee
      </a>
      (IOC) in 2001,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-winterhistory_10-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-winterhistory-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
       <a href="#cite_note-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and was recently extended through 2020.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc-firstglobal_13-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-bbc-firstglobal-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/1992_Winter_Paralympics" title="1992 Winter Paralympics">
       1992 Winter Paralympics
      </a>
      were the first Winter Games to use the same facilities as the
      <a href="/wiki/1992_Winter_Olympics" title="1992 Winter Olympics">
       Winter Olympics
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Winter_Games">
       Winter Games
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Paralympic_Games&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Winter Games">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Winter_Paralympic_Games" title="Winter Paralympic Games">
       Winter Paralympic Games
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      The first Winter Paralympic Games were held in
      <a href="/wiki/1976_Winter_Paralympics" title="1976 Winter Paralympics">
       1976
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/%C3%96rnsk%C3%B6ldsvik" title="rnskldsvik">
       rnskldsvik
      </a>
      , Sweden. This was the first Paralympics in which multiple categories of athletes with disabilities could compete.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-winterhistory_10-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-winterhistory-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Winter Games were celebrated every four years on the same year as their summer counterpart, just as the Olympics were. This tradition was upheld until the
      <a href="/wiki/1992_Winter_Paralympics" title="1992 Winter Paralympics">
       1992 Games
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Albertville" title="Albertville">
       Albertville
      </a>
      , France; after that, beginning with the
      <a href="/wiki/1994_Winter_Paralympics" title="1994 Winter Paralympics">
       1994 Games
      </a>
      , the Winter Paralympics and the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Winter_Olympics" title="Winter Olympics">
       Winter Olympics
      </a>
      have been held in those even numbered years separate from the Summer Games.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-winterhistory_10-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-winterhistory-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Recent_games">
       Recent games
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Paralympic_Games&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Recent games">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The Paralympic games were designed to emphasize the participants' athletic achievements and not their disability. Recent games have emphasized that these games are about ability and not disability.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aboutipc_14-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-aboutipc-14">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        14
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The movement has grown dramatically since its early days  for example, the number of athletes participating in the Summer Paralympic games has increased from 400 athletes in Rome in 1960 to over 3,900 athletes from 146 countries in Beijing in
      <a href="/wiki/2008_Summer_Paralympics" title="2008 Summer Paralympics">
       2008
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
       <a href="#cite_note-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Both the Paralympic Summer and Winter Games are recognized on the world stage. The Paralympics is no longer held solely for British war veterans or just for athletes in wheelchairs, but for elite athletes with a wide variety of disabilities from all over the world. The paralympics allow people with special needs a chance to heal their injuries and take part in the games.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
       <a href="#cite_note-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        16
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="International_Paralympic_Committee">
       International Paralympic Committee
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Paralympic_Games&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: International Paralympic Committee">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/International_Paralympic_Committee" title="International Paralympic Committee">
       International Paralympic Committee
      </a>
     </div>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Forerunners_.281964.E2.80.931989.29">
       Forerunners (19641989)
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Paralympic_Games&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Forerunners (19641989)">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The first organization dedicated to advancement of athletic opportunities for people with a disability was the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_Sports_Organization_for_the_Disabled" title="International Sports Organization for the Disabled">
       International Sports Organization for the Disabled
      </a>
      (ISOD), founded in 1964. The founders of this organization intended it to be a governing body to disability sports what the IOC was to the Olympic Games.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
       <a href="#cite_note-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This committee eventually became the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_Coordinating_Committee_of_World_Sports_Organizations_for_the_Disabled" title="International Coordinating Committee of World Sports Organizations for the Disabled">
       International Coordinating Committee of World Sports Organizations for the Disabled
      </a>
      (ICC), which was established in 1982. The ICC was tasked with advocating for the rights of athletes with a disability in front of the IOC.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Howe_2008.2C_p._43_18-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Howe_2008.2C_p._43-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      After the success of the cooperative effort between the ICC and the IOC, which resulted in the 1988 Summer Paralympics in Seoul, the ICC determined the need to expand and include representatives from all nations that had disability sports programs. They also deemed it necessary to include athletes in the decisions of the Paralympic governing body. Consequently this body was reorganized as the
      <a href="/wiki/International_Paralympic_Committee" title="International Paralympic Committee">
       International Paralympic Committee
      </a>
      (IPC) in 1989.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-winterhistory_10-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-winterhistory-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Howe_2008.2C_p._43_18-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Howe_2008.2C_p._43-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="IPC_.28since_1989.29">
       IPC (since 1989)
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Paralympic_Games&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: IPC (since 1989)">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Germany_Bonn_IPC_2009_06_28.jpg">
        <img alt="A white building with trees next to it fronting a street with a car driving past" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2420" data-file-width="3268" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Germany_Bonn_IPC_2009_06_28.jpg/220px-Germany_Bonn_IPC_2009_06_28.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Germany_Bonn_IPC_2009_06_28.jpg/330px-Germany_Bonn_IPC_2009_06_28.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Germany_Bonn_IPC_2009_06_28.jpg/440px-Germany_Bonn_IPC_2009_06_28.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Germany_Bonn_IPC_2009_06_28.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        IPC headquarters in
        <a href="/wiki/Bonn" title="Bonn">
         Bonn
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The IPC is the global governing body of the Paralympic Movement. It comprises 176
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_19-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-19">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        19
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      National Paralympic Committees (NPC) and four disability-specific international sports federations.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
       <a href="#cite_note-20">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The president of the IPC is
      <a href="/wiki/Philip_Craven" title="Philip Craven">
       Philip Craven
      </a>
      , a former Paralympian from Great Britain.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
       <a href="#cite_note-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In his capacity as head of the IPC, Craven is also a member of the International Olympic Committee.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
       <a href="#cite_note-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The IPC's international headquarters are in
      <a href="/wiki/Bonn" title="Bonn">
       Bonn
      </a>
      , Germany.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
       <a href="#cite_note-23">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        23
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The IPC is responsible for organizing the Summer and Winter Paralympic Games. It also serves as the International Federation for nine sports (
      <a href="/wiki/Paralympic_athletics" title="Paralympic athletics">
       Paralympic athletics
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Paralympic_swimming" title="Paralympic swimming">
       Paralympic swimming
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Paralympic_shooting" title="Paralympic shooting">
       Paralympic shooting
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Paralympic_powerlifting" title="Paralympic powerlifting">
       Paralympic powerlifting
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Para-alpine_skiing" title="Para-alpine skiing">
       Para-alpine skiing
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Paralympic_biathlon" title="Paralympic biathlon">
       Paralympic biathlon
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Paralympic_cross-country_skiing" title="Paralympic cross-country skiing">
       Paralympic cross-country skiing
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ice_sledge_hockey" title="Ice sledge hockey">
       ice sledge hockey
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Wheelchair_DanceSport" title="Wheelchair DanceSport">
       Wheelchair DanceSport
      </a>
      ). This requires the IPC to supervise and coordinate the World Championships and other competitions for each of the nine sports it regulates.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aboutipc_14-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-aboutipc-14">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        14
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      IPC membership also includes National Paralympic Committees
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_19-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-19">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        19
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and international sporting federations.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
       <a href="#cite_note-24">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        24
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      International Federations are independent sport federations recognized by the IPC as the sole representative of a Paralympic Sport. International Federations responsibilities include technical jurisdiction and guidance over the competition and training venues of their respective sports during the Paralympic Games. The IPC also recognizes media partners, certifies officials, judges, and is responsible for enforcing the bylaws of the
      <a href="/wiki/Paralympic_Charter" title="Paralympic Charter">
       Paralympic Charter
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-movement_25-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-movement-25">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        25
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The IPC has a cooperative relationship with the
      <a href="/wiki/International_Olympic_Committee" title="International Olympic Committee">
       International Olympic Committee
      </a>
      (IOC). Delegates of the IPC are also members of the IOC and participate on IOC committees and commissions. The two governing bodies remain distinct, with separate Games, despite the close working relationship.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
       <a href="#cite_note-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div style="clear:both;">
     </div>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Name_and_symbols">
       Name and symbols
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Paralympic_Games&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Name and symbols">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Paralympic-flag.jpg">
        <img alt="A flag with three red, gree, and blue swooshes on a white background.  It is attached to a flag pole and is framed by a blue sky" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2848" data-file-width="4272" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Paralympic-flag.jpg/220px-Paralympic-flag.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Paralympic-flag.jpg/330px-Paralympic-flag.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Paralympic-flag.jpg/440px-Paralympic-flag.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Paralympic-flag.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paralympic_flag" title="Paralympic flag">
         Paralympic flag
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Paralympic_symbols" title="Paralympic symbols">
       Paralympic symbols
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Although the name was originally coined as a portmanteau combining "paraplegic" (due to its origins as games for people with spinal injuries) and "Olympic," the inclusion of other disability groups meant that this was no longer considered very accurate. The present formal explanation for the name is that it derives from the Greek preposition ,
      <i>
       par
      </i>
      ("beside" or "alongside") and thus refers to a competition held in parallel with the
      <a href="/wiki/Olympic_Games" title="Olympic Games">
       Olympic Games
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HistoryofIPC_27-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-HistoryofIPC-27">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/1988_Summer_Paralympics" title="1988 Summer Paralympics">
       Summer Games of 1988
      </a>
      held in Seoul was the first time the term "Paralympic" came into official use.
     </p>
     <p>
      Spirit in Motion is the motto for the Paralympic movement. The symbol for the Paralympics contains three colours, red, blue, and green, which are the colours most widely represented in the flags of nations. The colours are each in the shape of an
      <i>
       Agito
      </i>
      (which is Latin for "I move"), which is the name given to an asymmetrical crescent specially designed for the Paralympic movement. The three Agitos circle a central point, which is a symbol for the athletes congregating from all points of the globe.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
       <a href="#cite_note-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        28
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The motto and symbol of the IPC were changed in 2003 to their current versions. The change was intended to convey the idea that Paralympians have a spirit of competition and that the IPC as an organization realizes its potential and is moving forward to achieve it. The vision of the IPC is, "To enable Paralympic athletes to achieve sporting excellence and to inspire and excite the world."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
       <a href="#cite_note-29">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        29
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Paralympic anthem is "Hymn de l'Avenir" or "Anthem of the Future". It was composed by
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Thierry_Darnis&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Thierry Darnis (page does not exist)">
       Thierry Darnis
      </a>
      and adopted as the official anthem in March 1996.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
       <a href="#cite_note-30">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        30
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Ceremonies">
       Ceremonies
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Paralympic_Games&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Ceremonies">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Opening">
       Opening
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Paralympic_Games&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Opening">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Paralympics_Opening_Ceremony.jpg">
        <img alt="A portion of the stadium with stands full of people, a large artificial tree is on the right side of the image.  A group of people are walking together on the stadium floor" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1039" data-file-width="1977" height="116" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Paralympics_Opening_Ceremony.jpg/220px-Paralympics_Opening_Ceremony.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Paralympics_Opening_Ceremony.jpg/330px-Paralympics_Opening_Ceremony.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Paralympics_Opening_Ceremony.jpg/440px-Paralympics_Opening_Ceremony.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Paralympics_Opening_Ceremony.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Opening ceremony of the
        <a href="/wiki/2004_Summer_Paralympics" title="2004 Summer Paralympics">
         2004 Summer Paralympics
        </a>
        in
        <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">
         Athens
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      As mandated by the Paralympic Charter, various elements frame the opening ceremony of the Paralympic Games. Most of these rituals were established at the
      <a href="/wiki/1920_Summer_Olympics" title="1920 Summer Olympics">
       1920 Summer Olympics
      </a>
      in Antwerp.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OCprotocol1_31-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-OCprotocol1-31">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The ceremony typically starts with the hoisting of the host country's flag and a performance of its national anthem. Unlike the Olympic Games, after the national anthem, the athletes parade into the stadium grouped by nation. Nations enter the stadium alphabetically according to the host country's chosen language, though with the host country's athletes being the last to enter. Then the host nation presents artistic displays of music, singing, dance, and theatre representative of its culture.
     </p>
     <p>
      Speeches are given, formally opening the games. Finally, the Paralympic torch is brought into the stadium and passed on until it reaches the final torch carrier  often a Paralympic athlete from the host nation  who lights the Paralympic flame in the stadium's cauldron.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
       <a href="#cite_note-32">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        32
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Closing">
       Closing
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Paralympic_Games&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Closing">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The closing ceremony of the Paralympic Games takes place after all sporting events have concluded. Flag-bearers from each participating country enter, followed by the athletes who enter together, without any national distinction. The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paralympic_flag" title="Paralympic flag">
       Paralympic flag
      </a>
      is taken down.Since the
      <a href="/wiki/2002_Winter_Paralympics" title="2002 Winter Paralympics">
       2002 Winter Paralympics
      </a>
      the national flag of the country hosting the next Summer or Winter Paralympic Games is hoisted while the corresponding national anthem is played. The games are officially closed, and the Paralympic flame is extinguished.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
       <a href="#cite_note-33">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        33
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      After these compulsory elements, the next host nation briefly introduces itself with artistic displays of dance and theater representative of its culture.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Medal_presentation">
       Medal presentation
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Paralympic_Games&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Medal presentation">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2010_Winter_Paralympics_Men%27s_Biathlon_pursuit_vi_medalists.jpg">
        <img alt="six men stand together wearing Paralympic medals and waving flower bouquets" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2670" data-file-width="3560" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/2010_Winter_Paralympics_Men%27s_Biathlon_pursuit_vi_medalists.jpg/220px-2010_Winter_Paralympics_Men%27s_Biathlon_pursuit_vi_medalists.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/2010_Winter_Paralympics_Men%27s_Biathlon_pursuit_vi_medalists.jpg/330px-2010_Winter_Paralympics_Men%27s_Biathlon_pursuit_vi_medalists.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/2010_Winter_Paralympics_Men%27s_Biathlon_pursuit_vi_medalists.jpg/440px-2010_Winter_Paralympics_Men%27s_Biathlon_pursuit_vi_medalists.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2010_Winter_Paralympics_Men%27s_Biathlon_pursuit_vi_medalists.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        A medal ceremony during the 2010 Winter Paralympics.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      A medal ceremony is held after each Paralympic event is concluded. The winner, second and third-place competitors or teams stand on top of a three-tiered rostrum to be awarded their respective medals. After the medals are given out by an IPC member, the national flags of the three medallists are raised while the
      <a href="/wiki/National_anthem" title="National anthem">
       national anthem
      </a>
      of the gold medallists country plays.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
       <a href="#cite_note-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Volunteering citizens of the host country also act as hosts during the medal ceremonies, as they aid the officials who present the medals and act as flag-bearers.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
       <a href="#cite_note-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      For every Paralympic event, the respective medal ceremony is held, at most, one day after the event's final.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Equality">
       Equality
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Paralympic_Games&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Equality">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Relationship_with_the_Olympics">
       Relationship with the Olympics
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Paralympic_Games&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Relationship with the Olympics">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      In 2001 the
      <a href="/wiki/International_Olympic_Committee" title="International Olympic Committee">
       International Olympic Committee
      </a>
      (IOC) and the
      <a href="/wiki/International_Paralympic_Committee" title="International Paralympic Committee">
       International Paralympic Committee
      </a>
      (IPC) signed an agreement which guaranteed that host cities would be contracted to manage both the Olympic and Paralympic Games. This agreement will remain in effect until the
      <a href="/wiki/2012_Summer_Olympics" title="2012 Summer Olympics">
       2012 Summer Olympics
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-winterhistory_10-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-winterhistory-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and will be further extended for all Summer and Winter games up until the
      <a href="/wiki/2020_Summer_Olympics" title="2020 Summer Olympics">
       2020 Summer Olympics
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-agreement_36-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-agreement-36">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The IOC has written its commitment to equal access to athletics for all people into its
      <a href="/wiki/Olympic_Charter" title="Olympic Charter">
       charter
      </a>
      , which states,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
       <a href="#cite_note-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        37
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <table class="cquote" role="presentation" style="margin:auto; border-collapse: collapse; border: none; background-color: transparent; width: auto;">
      <tr>
       <td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: top; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: left; padding: 10px 10px;">
        
       </td>
       <td style="vertical-align: top; border: none; padding: 4px 10px;">
        The practice of sport is a human right. Every individual must have the possibility of practising sport, without discrimination of any kind and in the Olympic spirit, which requires mutual understanding with a spirit of friendship, solidarity and fair play....Any form of discrimination with regard to a country or a person on grounds of race, religion, politics, gender or otherwise is incompatible with belonging to the Olympic Movement.
       </td>
       <td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: bottom; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: right; padding: 10px 10px;">
        
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      While the charter is silent on discrimination specifically related to disability; given the language in the charter regarding discrimination it is reasonable to infer that discrimination on the basis of disability would be against the ideals of the Olympic Charter and the IOC.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
       <a href="#cite_note-38">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        38
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This is also consistent with the Paralympic Charter, which forbids discrimination on the basis of political, religious, economic, disability, gender, sexual orientation or racial reasons.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
       <a href="#cite_note-39">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        39
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Chairman of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/London_organising_committee" title="London organising committee">
       London organising committee
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Sebastian_Coe" title="Sebastian Coe">
       Sebastian Coe
      </a>
      , said about the
      <a href="/wiki/2012_Summer_Paralympics" title="2012 Summer Paralympics">
       2012 Summer Paralympics
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/2012_Summer_Olympics" title="2012 Summer Olympics">
       2012 Summer Olympics
      </a>
      in London, England, that
     </p>
     <table class="cquote" role="presentation" style="margin:auto; border-collapse: collapse; border: none; background-color: transparent; width: auto;">
      <tr>
       <td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: top; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: left; padding: 10px 10px;">
        
       </td>
       <td style="vertical-align: top; border: none; padding: 4px 10px;">
        We want to change public attitudes towards disability, celebrate the excellence of Paralympic sport and to enshrine from the very outset that the two Games are an integrated whole.
       </td>
       <td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: bottom; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: right; padding: 10px 10px;">
        
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
       <a href="#cite_note-40">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        40
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2014_Winter_Paralympic_Games" title="2014 Winter Paralympic Games">
       2014 Winter Paralympic Games
      </a>
      is the first such Paralympics hosted by Russia. Russia ratified the UN Convention on the Rights of Persons with Disabilities during that period. Notably at 2010 Vancouver their
      <a href="/wiki/Russia_at_the_2010_Winter_Paralympics" title="Russia at the 2010 Winter Paralympics">
       Paralympic team
      </a>
      topped the medal table at the
      <a href="/wiki/2010_Winter_Paralympics" title="2010 Winter Paralympics">
       Winter Paralympics
      </a>
      , while their
      <a href="/wiki/Russia_at_the_2010_Winter_Olympics" title="Russia at the 2010 Winter Olympics">
       Olympic team
      </a>
      performed well below expectations at the
      <a href="/wiki/2010_Winter_Olympics" title="2010 Winter Olympics">
       Winter Olympics
      </a>
      . This led the media to highlight the contrast between the achievements of the country's Olympic and Paralympic delegations, despite the greater attention and funding awarded to the Olympic athletes.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
       <a href="#cite_note-41">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        41
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
       <a href="#cite_note-42">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        42
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russian_Federation" title="Russian Federation">
       Russian Federation
      </a>
      organizers of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2014_Winter_Paralympic_Games" title="2014 Winter Paralympic Games">
       2014 Winter Paralympic Games
      </a>
      have, since 2007, made efforts to make the host city Sochi more accessible.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_43-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-43">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        43
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Paralympians_at_the_Olympics">
       Paralympians at the Olympics
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Paralympic_Games&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Paralympians at the Olympics">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Oscar_Pistorius-2.jpg">
        <img alt="A man in a spandex singlet runs on a track.  He has two prosthetics below the knees" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="591" data-file-width="368" height="273" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Oscar_Pistorius-2.jpg/170px-Oscar_Pistorius-2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Oscar_Pistorius-2.jpg/255px-Oscar_Pistorius-2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Oscar_Pistorius-2.jpg/340px-Oscar_Pistorius-2.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Oscar_Pistorius-2.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Oscar_Pistorius" title="Oscar Pistorius">
         Oscar Pistorius
        </a>
        at a track meet on 8 July 2007
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_athletes_who_have_competed_in_the_Paralympics_and_Olympics" title="List of athletes who have competed in the Paralympics and Olympics">
       List of athletes who have competed in the Paralympics and Olympics
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paralympian" title="Paralympian">
       Paralympic athletes
      </a>
      have sought equal opportunities to compete at the Olympic Games. The precedent was set by
      <a href="/wiki/Neroli_Fairhall" title="Neroli Fairhall">
       Neroli Fairhall
      </a>
      , a Paralympic archer from
      <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_at_the_Paralympics" title="New Zealand at the Paralympics">
       New Zealand
      </a>
      , who competed at the
      <a href="/wiki/1984_Summer_Olympics" title="1984 Summer Olympics">
       1984 Summer Olympics
      </a>
      in Los Angeles.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes.com_44-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-NYTimes.com-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 2008
      <a href="/wiki/Oscar_Pistorius" title="Oscar Pistorius">
       Oscar Pistorius
      </a>
      , a
      <a href="/wiki/South_Africa_at_the_Paralympics" title="South Africa at the Paralympics">
       South African
      </a>
      sprinter, attempted to qualify for the 2008 Summer Olympics. Pistorius had both his legs amputated below the knee and races with two carbon fibre blades manufactured by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ossur" title="Ossur">
       Ossur
      </a>
      . He holds Paralympic world record in the 400meter event.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
       <a href="#cite_note-45">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        45
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Pistorius missed qualifying for the
      <a href="/wiki/2008_Summer_Olympics" title="2008 Summer Olympics">
       2008 Summer Olympics
      </a>
      in the 400meter race, by 0.70seconds. He qualified for the
      <a href="/wiki/2008_Summer_Paralympics" title="2008 Summer Paralympics">
       2008 Summer Paralympics
      </a>
      where he won gold medals in the 100, 200, and 400meter sprints.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
       <a href="#cite_note-46">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        46
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 2011, Pistorius qualified for the
      <a href="/wiki/2012_Summer_Olympics" title="2012 Summer Olympics">
       2012 Summer Olympics
      </a>
      and competed in two events: he made the semi-final in the
      <a href="/wiki/400_metres" title="400 metres">
       400 metres
      </a>
      race; and his team came 8th in the final of the
      <a href="/wiki/4_%C3%97_400_metres_relay" title="4  400 metres relay">
       4  400 metres relay
      </a>
      race.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
       <a href="#cite_note-47">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        47
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Some athletes without a disability also compete at the Paralympics; The
      <a href="/wiki/Sighted_guide" title="Sighted guide">
       sighted guides
      </a>
      for athletes with a visual impairment are such a close and essential part of the competition that the athlete with visual impairment and the guide are considered a team, and both athletes are medal candidates.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thestar.com_48-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-thestar.com-48">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        48
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Funding">
       Funding
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Paralympic_Games&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Funding">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      There has been criticism for not providing equal funding to Paralympic athletes as compared to Olympic athletes. An example of this criticism was a lawsuit filed by Paralympic athletes
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Tony_Iniguez&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Tony Iniguez (page does not exist)">
       Tony Iniguez
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Scot_Hollonbeck" title="Scot Hollonbeck">
       Scot Hollonbeck
      </a>
      and
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Jacob_Heilveil&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Jacob Heilveil (page does not exist)">
       Jacob Heilveil
      </a>
      of the
      <a href="/wiki/United_States_at_the_Paralympics" title="United States at the Paralympics">
       United States
      </a>
      , in 2003.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-court_49-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-court-49">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        49
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      They alleged that the
      <a href="/wiki/United_States_Olympic_Committee" title="United States Olympic Committee">
       United States Olympic Committee
      </a>
      (USOC), which also include the USOC Paralympic Division (the National Paralympic Committee), was underfunding American Paralympic athletes. Iniguez cited the fact that the USOC made healthcare benefits available to a smaller percentage of Paralympians, the USOC provided smaller quarterly training stipends and paid smaller financial awards for medals won at a Paralympics. US Paralympians saw this as a disadvantage for the US Paralympic athletes, as nations such as Canada and Britain supported Paralympians and Olympians virtually equally. The USOC did not deny the discrepancy in funding and contended that this was due to the fact that it did not receive any government financial support. As a result it had to rely on revenue generated by the media exposure of its athletes. Olympic athletic success resulted in greater exposure for the USOC than Paralympic athletic achievements. The case was heard by lower courts, who ruled that the USOC has the right to allocate its finances to athletes at different rates. The case was appealed to the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Supreme_Court" title="United States Supreme Court">
       Supreme Court
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
       <a href="#cite_note-50">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        50
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      where on September 6, 2008 it announced that it would not hear the appeal. However, during the time the lawsuit lasted (from 2003 to 2008), the funding from the USOC had nearly tripled. In 2008 $11.4million was earmarked for Paralympic athletes, up from $3million in 2004.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-court_49-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-court-49">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        49
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      As with the Olympics, recent Paralympics have also been supported by contributions from major sponsors. Unlike the Olympics, where the IOC mandates that arenas be clean of sponsor logos, the Paralympics do allow the logos of official sponsors to be displayed inside arenas and on uniforms.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-paralympic-twists_51-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-paralympic-twists-51">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        51
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Media_coverage">
       Media coverage
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Paralympic_Games&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Media coverage">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      While the Olympic Games have experienced tremendous growth in global media coverage since the
      <a href="/wiki/1984_Summer_Olympics" title="1984 Summer Olympics">
       1984 Summer Olympics
      </a>
      , the Paralympics have been unable to maintain a consistent international media presence.
     </p>
     <p>
      Television broadcasts of Paralympic Games began in 1976, but this early coverage was confined to taped-delay releases to one nation or region. At the 1992 Summer Paralympics there were 45hours of live coverage but it was available only in Europe. Other countries broadcast highlight packages during the Games. No meaningful improvements in coverage occurred until the
      <a href="/wiki/2000_Summer_Paralympics" title="2000 Summer Paralympics">
       2000 Summer Paralympics
      </a>
      in Sydney.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
       <a href="#cite_note-52">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        52
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The 2000 Paralympics represented a significant increase in global media exposure for the Paralympic Games. A deal was reached between the Sydney Paralympic Organizing Committee (SPOC) and All Media Sports (AMS) to broadcast the Games internationally. Deals were reached with Asian, South American, and European broadcast companies to distribute coverage to as many markets as possible. The Games were also webcast for the first time. Because of these efforts the Sydney Paralympics reached a global audience estimated at 300million people.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
       <a href="#cite_note-53">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        53
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Also significant was the fact that the organizers did not have to pay networks to televise the Games as had been done at the
      <a href="/wiki/1992_Summer_Paralympics" title="1992 Summer Paralympics">
       1992
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/1996_Summer_Paralympics" title="1996 Summer Paralympics">
       1996
      </a>
      Games.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
       <a href="#cite_note-54">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        54
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Despite these advances consistent media attention has been a challenge, which was evidenced in the coverage in Great Britain of the
      <a href="/wiki/2010_Winter_Paralympics" title="2010 Winter Paralympics">
       2010 Winter Paralympics
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_Broadcasting_Corporation" title="British Broadcasting Corporation">
       British Broadcasting Corporation
      </a>
      (BBC) was criticized for its minimal coverage of the 2010 Winter Paralympics as compared to its coverage of the
      <a href="/wiki/2010_Winter_Olympics" title="2010 Winter Olympics">
       2010 Winter Olympics
      </a>
      . The BBC announced it would stream some content on its website and show a one-hour highlight program after the Games ended. For the Winter Olympics the BBC aired 160 hours of coverage. The response from the BBC was that budget constraints and the "time zone factor" necessitated a limited broadcast schedule.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbcmedia_55-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-bbcmedia-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The reduction in coverage was done in spite of increased ratings for the
      <a href="/wiki/2008_Summer_Paralympics" title="2008 Summer Paralympics">
       2008 Summer Paralympics
      </a>
      , which was watched by 23% of the population of Great Britain.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbcmedia_55-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-bbcmedia-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In Norway, the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norwegian_Broadcasting_Corporation" title="Norwegian Broadcasting Corporation">
       Norwegian Broadcasting Corporation
      </a>
      (NRK) broadcast 30 hours of the 2010 Winter Games live. NRK-sport were critical of parts of the TV production from Vancouver, and notified the
      <a href="/wiki/European_Broadcasting_Union" title="European Broadcasting Union">
       EBU
      </a>
      of issues such as the
      <a href="/wiki/Paralympic_biathlon" title="Paralympic biathlon">
       biathlon
      </a>
      coverage excluding the shooting, and
      <a href="/wiki/Paralympic_cross-country_skiing" title="Paralympic cross-country skiing">
       cross-country skiing
      </a>
      with skiers in the distance, making it hard to follow the progress of the competition. NRK were far more pleased with the production of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ice_sledge_hockey" title="Ice sledge hockey">
       ice sledge hockey
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Wheelchair_curling" title="Wheelchair curling">
       wheelchair curling
      </a>
      events, which they felt reached the same level as the Olympic Games.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
       <a href="#cite_note-56">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Commercial broadcaster
      <a href="/wiki/Channel_4" title="Channel 4">
       Channel 4
      </a>
      acquired the rights to the Paralympics in the United Kingdom for the
      <a href="/wiki/2012_Summer_Paralympics" title="2012 Summer Paralympics">
       2012 Summer Paralympics
      </a>
      , and planned to air extensive coverage of the games; Channel 4 aired 150 hours of coverage, and also offered
      <a href="/wiki/Mobile_app" title="Mobile app">
       mobile apps
      </a>
      , and three dedicated streaming channels of additional coverage on
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sky_(UK_and_Ireland)" title="Sky (UK and Ireland)">
       Sky
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Freesat" title="Freesat">
       Freesat
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Virgin_Media" title="Virgin Media">
       Virgin Media
      </a>
      and Channel 4's website."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
       <a href="#cite_note-57">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        57
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Channel 4 also made a push to heighten the profile of the Paralympics in the country by producing a 2 minute trailer for its coverage, "Meet the Superhumans"; which premired simultaneously on over 70 commercial channels in the UK on 17 July 2012.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adott-c4paralympics_58-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-adott-c4paralympics-58">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        58
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ind-c4para_59-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ind-c4para-59">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        59
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Channel 4 have also acquired the rights to the
      <a href="/wiki/2014_Winter_Paralympics" title="2014 Winter Paralympics">
       2014 Winter Paralympics
      </a>
      and the
      <a href="/wiki/2016_Summer_Paralympics" title="2016 Summer Paralympics">
       2016 Summer Paralympics
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
       <a href="#cite_note-60">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        60
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">
       American
      </a>
      broadcaster
      <a href="/wiki/NBC_Sports" title="NBC Sports">
       NBC Sports
      </a>
      , who also owns the broadcast rights to the Olympics, has been criticised by athletes and IPC officials for airing only a bare minimum of coverage from the Paralympics; the lack of coverage from NBC in
      <a href="/wiki/2004_Summer_Paralympics" title="2004 Summer Paralympics">
       Athens
      </a>
      was a cause for concern from senior IPC officials, especially given that the United States was
      <a href="/wiki/New_York_City_bid_for_the_2012_Summer_Olympics" title="New York City bid for the 2012 Summer Olympics">
       bidding for the 2012 Games
      </a>
      . In 2012 NBC only produced around 5 hours of
      <a href="/wiki/Broadcast_delay" title="Broadcast delay">
       tape delayed
      </a>
      highlights from the Games, airing on the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pay_TV" title="Pay TV">
       pay TV
      </a>
      channel
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NBC_Sports_Network" title="NBC Sports Network">
       NBC Sports Network
      </a>
      , and did not cover the ceremonies at all.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbc-para_61-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-nbc-para-61">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        61
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      IPC president Philip Craven was vocal about NBC's reluctance to air coverage in 2012, expressing his disappointment for American athletes and viewers who would miss the "amazing images" the games would bring, and remarking that "some people think that North America always lead[s] on everything, and on this they don't. It's about time they caught up."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tele-nbc_62-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-tele-nbc-62">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        62
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Following the closing ceremonies, Craven hinted that the IPC might greater scrutiny on broadcasters at future editions of the Paralympics (or may strip NBC of its broadcast rights), by stating that "if we find our
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Values" title="Values">
       values
      </a>
      dont fit, well have to go somewhere else."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-metro-nbc_63-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-metro-nbc-63">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        63
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      NBC would pick up broadcast rights to the 2014 and 2016 Paralympics, promising a significantly larger amount of coverage than before.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-telegraph-nbcparalympics_64-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-telegraph-nbcparalympics-64">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        64
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Outside_the_games">
       Outside the games
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Paralympic_Games&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Outside the games">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      A 2010 study by the
      <a href="/wiki/University_of_British_Columbia" title="University of British Columbia">
       University of British Columbia
      </a>
      (UBC) on the Olympic Games Impact (OGI), showed that of roughly 1,600 Canadian respondents, 4150 percent believed the
      <a href="/wiki/2010_Winter_Paralympics" title="2010 Winter Paralympics">
       2010 Paralympic
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/2010_Winter_Olympics" title="2010 Winter Olympics">
       Olympic Games
      </a>
      in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vancouver,_Canada" title="Vancouver, Canada">
       Vancouver, Canada
      </a>
      triggered additional accessibility of buildings, sidewalks and public spaces. 23 percent of employers said the Games had increased their willingness to hire people with disabilities.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
       <a href="#cite_note-65">
