        <span>
         [
        </span>
        65
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Chief Executive Officer for the International Paralympic Committee, Xavier Gonzalez, said about the
      <a href="/wiki/2008_Summer_Paralympics" title="2008 Summer Paralympics">
       2008 Summer Paralympics
      </a>
      in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beijing,_China" title="Beijing, China">
       Beijing
      </a>
      , China, that
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
       <a href="#cite_note-66">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        66
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <table class="cquote" role="presentation" style="margin:auto; border-collapse: collapse; border: none; background-color: transparent; width: auto;">
      <tr>
       <td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: top; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: left; padding: 10px 10px;">
        
       </td>
       <td style="vertical-align: top; border: none; padding: 4px 10px;">
        In China, the (Paralympic) Games were really a transformation tool for changing attitudes across the board in China towards people with disability, to building accessibility facilities in the city, to changing laws to allow people with a disability to be part of society.
       </td>
       <td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: bottom; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: right; padding: 10px 10px;">
        
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">
       Classification
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Paralympic_Games&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Classification">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Disability_sport_classification" title="Disability sport classification">
       Disability sport classification
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Paralympic_XC_ski_sitting.JPG">
        <img alt="A woman sitting on sit-skis, she is pushing herself with two poles" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1168" data-file-width="1562" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Paralympic_XC_ski_sitting.JPG/220px-Paralympic_XC_ski_sitting.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Paralympic_XC_ski_sitting.JPG/330px-Paralympic_XC_ski_sitting.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Paralympic_XC_ski_sitting.JPG/440px-Paralympic_XC_ski_sitting.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Paralympic_XC_ski_sitting.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Olena_Iurkovska" title="Olena Iurkovska">
         Olena Iurkovska
        </a>
        of
        <a href="/wiki/Ukraine_at_the_2010_Winter_Paralympics" title="Ukraine at the 2010 Winter Paralympics">
         Ukraine
        </a>
        competing on cross-country sit-skis at the 2010 Winter Paralympics.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/International_Paralympic_Committee" title="International Paralympic Committee">
       International Paralympic Committee
      </a>
      (IPC) has established ten disability categories. Athletes are divided within each category according to their level of impairment, in a functional classification system which differs from sport to sport.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Categories">
       Categories
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Paralympic_Games&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Categories">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The IPC has established ten disability categories, including physical, visual, and intellectual impairment. Athletes with one of these disabilities can compete in the Paralympics though not every sport can allow for every disability category. These categories apply to both Summer and Winter Paralympics.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-paralympic.org_67-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-paralympic.org-67">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        67
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <b>
       Physical Impairment
      </b>
      - There are eight different types of physical impairment:
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <b>
        Impaired muscle power
       </b>
       - With impairments in this category, the force generated by muscles, such as the muscles of one limb, one side of the body or the lower half of the body is reduced, e.g. due to spinal-cord injury,
       <a href="/wiki/Spina_bifida" title="Spina bifida">
        spina bifida
       </a>
       or
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polio" title="Polio">
        polio
       </a>
       .
      </li>
      <li>
       <b>
        Impaired passive range of movement
       </b>
       - Range of movement in one or more joints is reduced in a systematic way. Acute conditions such as
       <a href="/wiki/Arthritis" title="Arthritis">
        arthritis
       </a>
       are not included.
      </li>
      <li>
       <b>
        Loss of limb or limb deficiency
       </b>
       - A total or partial absence of bones or joints from
       <a href="/wiki/Amputation" title="Amputation">
        partial or total loss
       </a>
       due to illness, trauma, or congenital limb deficiency (e.g.
       <a href="/wiki/Dysmelia" title="Dysmelia">
        dysmelia
       </a>
       ).
      </li>
      <li>
       <b>
        Leg-length difference
       </b>
       - Significant bone shortening occurs in one leg due to congenital deficiency or trauma.
      </li>
      <li>
       <b>
        Short stature
       </b>
       - Standing height is reduced due to shortened legs, arms and trunk, which are due to a musculoskeletal deficit of bone or cartilage structures.
      </li>
      <li>
       <b>
        <a href="/wiki/Hypertonia" title="Hypertonia">
         Hypertonia
        </a>
       </b>
       - Hypertonia is marked by an abnormal increase in muscle tension and reduced ability of a muscle to stretch. Hypertonia may result from injury, disease, or conditions which involve damage to the central nervous system (e.g. cerebral palsy).
      </li>
      <li>
       <b>
        <a href="/wiki/Ataxia" title="Ataxia">
         Ataxia
        </a>
       </b>
       - Ataxia is an impairment that consists of a lack of coordination of muscle movements (e.g.
       <a href="/wiki/Cerebral_palsy" title="Cerebral palsy">
        cerebral palsy
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Friedreich%E2%80%99s_ataxia" title="Friedreichs ataxia">
        Friedreichs ataxia
       </a>
       ).
      </li>
      <li>
       <b>
        <a href="/wiki/Athetosis" title="Athetosis">
         Athetosis
        </a>
       </b>
       - Athetosis is generally characterized by unbalanced, involuntary movements and a difficulty maintaining a symmetrical posture (e.g.
       <a href="/wiki/Cerebral_palsy" title="Cerebral palsy">
        cerebral palsy
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Choreoathetosis" title="Choreoathetosis">
        choreoathetosis
       </a>
       ).
      </li>
     </ul>
     <p>
      <b>
       Visual Impairment
      </b>
      - Athletes with
      <a href="/wiki/Visual_impairment" title="Visual impairment">
       visual impairment
      </a>
      ranging from partial vision, sufficient to be judged
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Legal_blindness" title="Legal blindness">
       legally blind
      </a>
      , to total
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blindness" title="Blindness">
       blindness
      </a>
      . This includes impairment of one or more component of the visual system (eye structure, receptors, optic nerve pathway, and visual cortex).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-paralympic.org_67-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-paralympic.org-67">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        67
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Sighted_guide" title="Sighted guide">
       sighted guides
      </a>
      for athletes with a visual impairment are such a close and essential part of the competition that the athlete with visual impairment and the guide are considered a team. Beginning in 2012, these guides (along with sighted
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Goalkeepers" title="Goalkeepers">
       goalkeepers
      </a>
      in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/5-a-side_football" title="5-a-side football">
       5-a-side football
      </a>
      became eligible to receive medals of their own.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thestar.com_48-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-thestar.com-48">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        48
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc-ablebodied_68-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-bbc-ablebodied-68">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        68
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <b>
       Intellectual Disability
      </b>
      - Athletes with a significant
      <a href="/wiki/Developmental_disability" title="Developmental disability">
       impairment in intellectual functioning
      </a>
      and associated limitations in adaptive behaviour. The IPC primarily serves athletes with physical disabilities, but the disability group Intellectual Disability has been added to some Paralympic Games. This includes only elite athletes with intellectual disabilities diagnosed before the age of 18.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-paralympic.org_67-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-paralympic.org-67">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        67
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IOC" title="IOC">
       IOC
      </a>
      -recognized
      <a href="/wiki/Special_Olympics_World_Games" title="Special Olympics World Games">
       Special Olympics World Games
      </a>
      are open to all people with intellectual disabilities.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-specialolympics_2-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-specialolympics-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-categories_69-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-categories-69">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        69
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Classification_system">
       Classification system
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Paralympic_Games&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Classification system">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Within the disability categories the athletes still need to be divided according to level of impairment. The classification systems differ from sport to sport and are intended to open up sports to as many athletes as possible who can participate in fair competitions against athletes with similar levels of ability. The biggest challenge in the classification system is how to account for the wide variety and severity of disabilities. Consequently, there is a range of impairment within most classifications .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-classdescribed_70-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-classdescribed-70">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        70
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Medical_classification_.28until_1980s.29">
       Medical classification (until 1980s)
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Paralympic_Games&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Medical classification (until 1980s)">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      From its inception until the 1980s, the Paralympic system for classifying athletes consisted of a medical evaluation and diagnosis of impairment. An athlete's medical condition was the only factor used to determine what class they competed in. For example an athlete who had a spinal cord injury that resulted in lower limb paresis, would not compete in the same wheelchair race as an athlete with a double above-knee amputation. The fact that their disability caused the same impairment did not factor into classification determination, the only consideration was their medical diagnosis. It was not until views on disabled athletics shifted from just a form of rehabilitation to an end in itself, that the classification system changed from medical diagnosis to a focus on the functional abilities of the athlete.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-classification_71-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-classification-71">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        71
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Functional_classification_.28since_1980s.29">
       Functional classification (since 1980s)
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Paralympic_Games&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Functional classification (since 1980s)">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Goalball_vid_Paralympics_i_Aten.jpg">
        <img alt="Three men wearing eye shades laying on the floor, a red ball is to the left of the image" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="3072" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Goalball_vid_Paralympics_i_Aten.jpg/220px-Goalball_vid_Paralympics_i_Aten.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Goalball_vid_Paralympics_i_Aten.jpg/330px-Goalball_vid_Paralympics_i_Aten.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Goalball_vid_Paralympics_i_Aten.jpg/440px-Goalball_vid_Paralympics_i_Aten.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Goalball_vid_Paralympics_i_Aten.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The Swedish goalball team at the
        <a href="/wiki/2004_Summer_Paralympics" title="2004 Summer Paralympics">
         2004 Summer Paralympics
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      While there is no clear date when the shift occurred, a functional classification system became the norm for disabled athletic classification in the 1980s. In a functional system the focus is on what impact the athlete's impairment has on his or her athletic performance. Under this system, athletes with total loss of function in their legs will compete together in most sports, because their functional loss is the same and the reason for the loss is immaterial. The only exception to the functional system is the classification format used by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_Blind_Sport_Federation" title="International Blind Sport Federation">
       International Blind Sport Federation
      </a>
      (IBSF0), which still uses a medically based system.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-classification_71-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-classification-71">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        71
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Some sports are only held for certain disability types. For example,
      <a href="/wiki/Goalball_at_the_Summer_Paralympics" title="Goalball at the Summer Paralympics">
       goalball
      </a>
      is only for visually impaired athletes. The Paralympics recognizes three different grades of visual impairment, consequently all competitors in goalball must wear a visor or "black out mask" so that athletes with less visual impairment will not have an advantage.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
       <a href="#cite_note-72">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        72
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Other sports, like
      <a href="/wiki/Athletics_at_the_Summer_Paralympics" title="Athletics at the Summer Paralympics">
       athletics
      </a>
      , are open to athletes with a wide variety of impairments. In athletics participants are broken down into a range of classes based on the disability they have and then they are placed in a classification within that range based on their level of impairment. For example: classes 1113 are for visually impaired athletes, which class they are in depends on their level of visual impairment.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
       <a href="#cite_note-73">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        73
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      There are also team competitions such as
      <a href="/wiki/Wheelchair_rugby_at_the_Summer_Paralympics" title="Wheelchair rugby at the Summer Paralympics">
       wheelchair rugby
      </a>
      . Members of the team are each given a point value based on their activity limitation. A lower score indicates a more severe activity limitation than a higher score. A team cannot have more than a certain maximum total of points on the field of play at the same time to ensure equal competition. For example, in wheelchair rugby the five players' combined disability number must total no more than eight points.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
       <a href="#cite_note-74">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        74
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Sports">
       Sports
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Paralympic_Games&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Sports">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Paralympic_sports" title="Paralympic sports">
       Paralympic sports
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      There are twenty-two sports on the Summer Paralympic program and five sports on the Winter Paralympics program. Within some of the sports are several events. For example,
      <a href="/wiki/Alpine_skiing_at_the_Winter_Paralympics" title="Alpine skiing at the Winter Paralympics">
       alpine skiing
      </a>
      has downhill, super combined, super-G, slalom, giant slalom. The IPC has governance over several of the sports but not all of them. Other international organizations, known as International Sports Federations (IF), notably the
      <a href="/wiki/International_Wheelchair_and_Amputee_Sports_Federation" title="International Wheelchair and Amputee Sports Federation">
       International Wheelchair and Amputee Sports Federation
      </a>
      (IWAS), the
      <a href="/wiki/International_Blind_Sports_Federation" title="International Blind Sports Federation">
       International Blind Sports Federation
      </a>
      (IBSA), and the
      <a href="/wiki/Cerebral_Palsy_International_Sports_and_Recreation_Association" title="Cerebral Palsy International Sports and Recreation Association">
       Cerebral Palsy International Sports and Recreation Association
      </a>
      (CP-ISRA), govern some sports that are specific to certain disability groups.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
       <a href="#cite_note-75">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        75
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      There are national chapters for these International Sport Federations including National Paralympic Committees, which are responsible for recruitment of athletes and governance of sports at the national level.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
       <a href="#cite_note-76">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        76
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Controversy">
       Controversy
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Paralympic_Games&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Controversy">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Cheating">
       Cheating
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Paralympic_Games&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Cheating">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Cheating_at_the_Paralympic_Games" title="Cheating at the Paralympic Games">
       Cheating at the Paralympic Games
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      After the
      <a href="/wiki/2000_Summer_Paralympics" title="2000 Summer Paralympics">
       2000 Sydney Games
      </a>
      , a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spain_at_the_Paralympic_Games" title="Spain at the Paralympic Games">
       Spanish
      </a>
      basketball player alleged that several members of the gold-medal winning Spanish basketball intellectually disabled (ID) team were not disabled. He claimed that only two athletes out of the twelve-member team met the qualifications of an intellectually disabled athlete.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cheating2_77-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-cheating2-77">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        77
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A controversy ensued and the IPC called on the Spanish National Paralympic Committee to launch an investigation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
       <a href="#cite_note-78">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        78
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The investigation uncovered several Spanish athletes who had flouted the ID rules. In an interview with the president of the federation that oversees ID competition, Fernando Martin Vicente admitted that athletes around the world were breaking the ID eligibility rules. The IPC responded by starting an investigation of its own.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cheating2_77-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-cheating2-77">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        77
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The results of the IPC's investigation confirmed the Spanish athlete's allegations and also determined that the incident was not isolated to the basketball ID event or to Spanish athletes.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cheating2_77-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-cheating2-77">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        77
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      As a result all ID competitions were suspended indefinitely.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
       <a href="#cite_note-79">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        79
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The ban was lifted after the 2008 Games after work had been done to tighten the criteria and controls governing admission of athletes with intellectual disabilities. Four sports, swimming, athletics, table tennis and rowing, were anticipated to hold competitions for ID athletes at the
      <a href="/wiki/2012_Summer_Paralympics" title="2012 Summer Paralympics">
       2012 Summer Paralympics
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cheating_80-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-cheating-80">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        80
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81">
       <a href="#cite_note-81">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        81
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The Paralympics have also been tainted by steroid use. At the 2008 Games in Beijing, three powerlifters and a German basketball player were banned after having tested positive for banned substances.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cheating_80-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-cheating-80">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        80
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This was a decrease in comparison to the ten powerlifters and one track athlete who were banned from the 2000 Games.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-steroids_82-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-steroids-82">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        82
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      German skier Thomas Oelsner became the first Winter Paralympian to test positive for steroids. He had won two gold medals at the
      <a href="/wiki/2002_Winter_Paralympics" title="2002 Winter Paralympics">
       2002 Winter Paralympics
      </a>
      , but his medals were stripped after his positive drug test.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
       <a href="#cite_note-83">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        83
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      At the 2010 Winter Olympics in Vancouver,
      <a href="/wiki/Sweden_at_the_2010_Winter_Paralympics" title="Sweden at the 2010 Winter Paralympics">
       Swedish
      </a>
      curler
      <a href="/wiki/Glenn_Ikonen" title="Glenn Ikonen">
       Glenn Ikonen
      </a>
      tested positive for a banned substance and was suspended for six months
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84">
       <a href="#cite_note-84">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        84
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      by the IPC. He was removed from the rest of the curling competition but his team was allowed to continue. The 54-year-old curler said his doctor had prescribed a medication on the banned substances list.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">
       <a href="#cite_note-85">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        85
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86">
       <a href="#cite_note-86">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        86
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Another concern now facing Paralympic officials is the technique of "
      <a href="/wiki/Boosting_(doping)" title="Boosting (doping)">
       boosting
      </a>
      ". Athletes can artificially increase their blood pressure, often by self-harming, which has been shown to improve performance by up to 15%. This is most effective in the endurance sports such as
      <a href="/wiki/Paralympic_cross-country_skiing" title="Paralympic cross-country skiing">
       cross-country skiing
      </a>
      . To increase blood pressure athletes will deliberately cause trauma to limbs below a spinal injury. This trauma can include breaking bones, strapping extremities in too tightly, and using high-pressured compression stockings. The injury is painless but it does have an impact on the athlete's blood pressure.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87">
       <a href="#cite_note-87">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        87
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Another potential concern is the use of
      <a href="/wiki/Gene_therapy" title="Gene therapy">
       gene therapy
      </a>
      among Paralympic athletes. All Paralympic athletes are banned from enhancing their abilities through
      <a href="/wiki/Gene_doping" title="Gene doping">
       gene doping
      </a>
      , but it is extremely difficult to differentiate these concepts.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Calgary_88-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Calgary-88">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        88
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The World Anti-Doping Agency is currently researching both gene doping and gene therapy, in part to discern the boundary between the two closely related concepts.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WADA_89-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-WADA-89">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        89
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The IPC have been working with the World Anti-Doping Agency since 2003, to ensure compliance with WADA's anti-doping code among its Paralympic athletes.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PlayTrue_90-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-PlayTrue-90">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        90
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The IPC has also promised to continue increasing the number of athletes tested at each of its Games, in order to further minimize the possible impact of doping in Paralympic sports.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PlayTrue_90-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-PlayTrue-90">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        90
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Mandatory in- and out-of competition testing has also been implemented by the IPC to further ensure all of its athletes are performing in compliance with WADA regulations.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PlayTrue_90-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-PlayTrue-90">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        90
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Notable_champions_and_achievements">
       Notable champions and achievements
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Paralympic_Games&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Notable champions and achievements">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote">
      Further information:
      <a href="/wiki/Lists_of_Paralympic_medalists" title="Lists of Paralympic medalists">
       Lists of Paralympic medalists
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_multiple_Paralympic_gold_medalists" title="List of multiple Paralympic gold medalists">
       List of multiple Paralympic gold medalists
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Trischa_Zorn" title="Trischa Zorn">
       Trischa Zorn
      </a>
      of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_at_the_Paralympic_Games" title="United States at the Paralympic Games">
       United States
      </a>
      is the most decorated Paralympian in history. She competed in the blind swimming events and won a total of 55 medals, 41 of which are gold. Her Paralympic career spanned 24years from
      <a href="/wiki/1980_Summer_Paralympics" title="1980 Summer Paralympics">
       1980
      </a>
      to
      <a href="/wiki/2004_Summer_Paralympics" title="2004 Summer Paralympics">
       2004
      </a>
      . She was also an alternate on the 1980 American Olympic swim team, but did not go to the
      <a href="/wiki/1980_Summer_Olympics" title="1980 Summer Olympics">
       Olympics
      </a>
      due to a
      <a href="/wiki/1980_Summer_Olympics_boycott" title="1980 Summer Olympics boycott">
       boycott
      </a>
      by the United States and several of its allies.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91">
       <a href="#cite_note-91">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        91
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92">
       <a href="#cite_note-92">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        92
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Ragnhild_Myklebust" title="Ragnhild Myklebust">
       Ragnhild Myklebust
      </a>
      of
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Norway_at_the_Paralympic_Games&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Norway at the Paralympic Games (page does not exist)">
       Norway
      </a>
      holds the record for the most medals ever won at the Winter Paralympic Games. Competing in a variety of events in 1988, 1992, 1994 and 2002, she won a total of 22 medals, of which 17 were gold. After winning five gold medals at the 2002 Games she retired at the age of 58.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93">
       <a href="#cite_note-93">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        93
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Neroli Fairhall, a paraplegic archer from New Zealand, was the first paraplegic competitor, and the first Paralympian, to participate in the Olympic Games, when she competed in the
      <a href="/wiki/1984_Summer_Olympics" title="1984 Summer Olympics">
       1984 Summer Olympics
      </a>
      in Los Angeles. She placed thirty-fourth in the Olympic archery competition, and won a Paralympic gold medal in the same event.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes.com_44-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-NYTimes.com-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
